<meta name="DC.Title" content="The Tarenaya hassleriana Genome Provides Insight into Reproductive Trait and Genome Evolution of Crucifers" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1105/tpc.113.113480" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2013-08-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2013 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.. Open Access articles can be viewed online without a subscription." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="The Brassicaceae, including Arabidopsis thaliana and Brassica crops, is unmatched among plants in its wealth of genomic and functional molecular data and has long served as a model for understanding gene, genome, and trait evolution. However, genome information from a phylogenetic outgroup that is essential for inferring directionality of evolutionary change has been lacking. We therefore sequenced the genome of the spider flower ( Tarenaya hassleriana ) from the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. By comparative analysis of the two lineages, we show that genome evolution following ancient polyploidy and gene duplication events affect reproductively important traits. We found an ancient genome triplication in Tarenaya (Th-α) that is independent of the Brassicaceae-specific duplication (At-α) and nested Brassica (Br-α) triplication. To showcase the potential of sister lineage genome analysis, we investigated the state of floral developmental genes and show Brassica retains twice as many floral MADS (for MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR) genes as Tarenaya that likely contribute to morphological diversity in Brassica . We also performed synteny analysis of gene families that confer self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae and found that the critical SERINE RECEPTOR KINASE receptor gene is derived from a lineage-specific tandem duplication. The T. hassleriana genome will facilitate future research toward elucidating the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae genomes.

*   ### Glossary
    
    SI
    :   self-incompatibility
    WGP
    :   whole-genome profiling
    TE
    :   transposable element
    qRT-PCR
    :   quantitative RT-PCR
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;The Brassicaceae, including &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Brassica&lt;/i&gt; crops, is unmatched among plants in its wealth of genomic and functional molecular data and has long served as a model for understanding gene, genome, and trait evolution. However, genome information from a phylogenetic outgroup that is essential for inferring directionality of evolutionary change has been lacking. We therefore sequenced the genome of the spider flower (&lt;i&gt;Tarenaya hassleriana&lt;/i&gt;) from the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. By comparative analysis of the two lineages, we show that genome evolution following ancient polyploidy and gene duplication events affect reproductively important traits. We found an ancient genome triplication in &lt;i&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/i&gt; (Th-α) that is independent of the Brassicaceae-specific duplication (At-α) and nested &lt;i&gt;Brassica&lt;/i&gt; (Br-α) triplication. To showcase the potential of sister lineage genome analysis, we investigated the state of floral developmental genes and show &lt;i&gt;Brassica&lt;/i&gt; retains twice as many floral MADS (for MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR) genes as &lt;i&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/i&gt; that likely contribute to morphological diversity in &lt;i&gt;Brassica&lt;/i&gt;. We also performed synteny analysis of gene families that confer self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae and found that the critical &lt;i&gt;SERINE RECEPTOR KINASE&lt;/i&gt; receptor gene is derived from a lineage-specific tandem duplication. The &lt;i&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/i&gt; genome will facilitate future research toward elucidating the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae genomes.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="The Tarenaya hassleriana Genome Provides Insight into Reproductive Trait and Genome Evolution of Crucifers" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantcell/25/8.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="The Brassicaceae, including Arabidopsis thaliana and Brassica crops, is unmatched among plants in its wealth of genomic and functional molecular data and has long served as a model for understanding gene, genome, and trait evolution. However, genome information from a phylogenetic outgroup that is essential for inferring directionality of evolutionary change has been lacking. We therefore sequenced the genome of the spider flower ( Tarenaya hassleriana ) from the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. By comparative analysis of the two lineages, we show that genome evolution following ancient polyploidy and gene duplication events affect reproductively important traits. We found an ancient genome triplication in Tarenaya (Th-α) that is independent of the Brassicaceae-specific duplication (At-α) and nested Brassica (Br-α) triplication. To showcase the potential of sister lineage genome analysis, we investigated the state of floral developmental genes and show Brassica retains twice as many floral MADS (for MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR) genes as Tarenaya that likely contribute to morphological diversity in Brassica . We also performed synteny analysis of gene families that confer self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae and found that the critical SERINE RECEPTOR KINASE receptor gene is derived from a lineage-specific tandem duplication. The T. hassleriana genome will facilitate future research toward elucidating the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae genomes.

*   ### Glossary
    
    SI
    :   self-incompatibility
    WGP
    :   whole-genome profiling
    TE
    :   transposable element
    qRT-PCR
    :   quantitative RT-PCR
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information" />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="The Brassicaceae, including Arabidopsis thaliana and Brassica crops, is unmatched among plants in its wealth of genomic and functional molecular data and has long served as a model for understanding gene, genome, and trait evolution. However, genome information from a phylogenetic outgroup that is essential for inferring directionality of evolutionary change has been lacking. We therefore sequenced the genome of the spider flower ( Tarenaya hassleriana ) from the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. By comparative analysis of the two lineages, we show that genome evolution following ancient polyploidy and gene duplication events affect reproductively important traits. We found an ancient genome triplication in Tarenaya (Th-α) that is independent of the Brassicaceae-specific duplication (At-α) and nested Brassica (Br-α) triplication. To showcase the potential of sister lineage genome analysis, we investigated the state of floral developmental genes and show Brassica retains twice as many floral MADS (for MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR) genes as Tarenaya that likely contribute to morphological diversity in Brassica . We also performed synteny analysis of gene families that confer self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae and found that the critical SERINE RECEPTOR KINASE receptor gene is derived from a lineage-specific tandem duplication. The T. hassleriana genome will facilitate future research toward elucidating the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae genomes.

  <div id="zone-content" class="zone zone-content clearfix container-30">    
        
    <div class="grid-28 suffix-1 prefix-1 region region-content" id="region-content">
  <div class="region-inner region-content-inner">
    <a id="main-content"></a>
                        <div class="block block-system block-main block-system-main odd block-without-title" id="block-system-main">
  <div class="block-inner clearfix">
                
    <div class="content clearfix">
      <div class="panel-display panels-960-layout jcore-2col-layout" >
	  <div class="panel-row-wrapper panel-row-first clearfix">
		
		<div class="top-wrapper">
			<div class="panel-panel panel-region-top">
			  <div class="inside"><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-article-crossmark" >

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantcell.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2013 American Society of Plant Biologists. All rights reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-8">The Brassicaceae, including <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> and <em>Brassica</em> crops, is unmatched among plants in its wealth of genomic and functional molecular data and has long served as a model for understanding gene, genome, and trait evolution. However, genome information from a phylogenetic outgroup that is essential for inferring directionality of evolutionary change has been lacking. We therefore sequenced the genome of the spider flower (<em>Tarenaya hassleriana</em>) from the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. By comparative analysis of the two lineages, we show that genome evolution following ancient polyploidy and gene duplication events affect reproductively important traits. We found an ancient genome triplication in <em>Tarenaya</em> (Th-α) that is independent of the Brassicaceae-specific duplication (At-α) and nested <em>Brassica</em> (Br-α) triplication. To showcase the potential of sister lineage genome analysis, we investigated the state of floral developmental genes and show <em>Brassica</em> retains twice as many floral MADS (for MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR) genes as <em>Tarenaya</em> that likely contribute to morphological diversity in <em>Brassica</em>. We also performed synteny analysis of gene families that confer self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae and found that the critical <em>SERINE RECEPTOR KINASE</em> receptor gene is derived from a lineage-specific tandem duplication. The <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome will facilitate future research toward elucidating the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae genomes.</p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-14">Studies of the model plant <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> and its close relatives in the Brassicaceae family have provided fundamental insight into the processes and patterns of plant evolution and function (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Koornneef and Meinke, 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Hu et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>). Comparative analyses between Brassicaceae and crop species have had profound influences on plant improvement and production. For example, knowledge about the control and evolution of plant reproductive traits, such as floral and fruit development and self-incompatibility (<a id="xref-def-1-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a>) systems, can be directly related to plant fitness and yield (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Tanksley, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Shen et al., 2005</a>). The Brassicaceae have also been a model for understanding the dynamics and impacts of ancient polyploidy (genome doubling), considering that the entire family has undergone a whole-genome duplication (named At-α) and the <em>Brassica</em> crops have had an additional genome triplication (Br-α) (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Blanc et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">Thomas et al., 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-99-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>). Genes retained in multiple copies due to these ancient polyploidy events, in addition to more recent tandem duplications, have played important roles in the evolution and regulation of key traits (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Edger and Pires, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Flagel and Wendel, 2009</a>). However, the polyploid history of the Brassicaceae also complicates synteny and evolutionary inferences to distantly related crop species.</p><p id="p-15">To fully exploit the fundamental trait and genome insights garnered from Brassicaceae systems and improve synteny analyses to more distant crops, we report the genome sequencing and analysis of <em>Tarenaya hassleriana</em> from the Brassicaceae sister family Cleomaceae. Currently, papaya (<em>Carica papaya</em>), a member of the order Brassicales, is the closest relative with a complete genome sequence; however, these two lineages diverged more than 70 to 110 million years ago (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Ming et al., 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Beilstein et al., 2010</a>). The Cleomaceae is the phylogenetic sister family to the Brassicaceae, with the two lineages having diverged only ∼38 million years ago (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Schranz and Mitchell-Olds, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Couvreur et al., 2010</a>). Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae share many traits (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Hall et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Iltis et al., 2011</a>), such as a preponderance of herbaceous species, the same general floral ground plan (four sepals, four petals, six stamens, and two fused carpels), and a replum in the mostly dehiscent fruits, referred to as capsules. There are also a number of key differences. Most of the 300 Cleomaceae species are restricted to the semitropics and arid desert regions and lack a genetic pollen-pistil <a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> system, whereas most of the 3700 Brassicaceae species largely radiated into cold temperate regions and possess a genetically regulated <a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> system (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Guo et al., 2011</a>). Another striking distinction is in floral symmetry: Cleomaceae have mostly monosymmetric flowers and Brassicaceae have mostly disymmetric flowers (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Endress, 1999</a>; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Patchell et al., 2011</a>). Cleomaceae also exhibit greater variation in the basic floral plan with increases in stamen number, petal dimorphisms, and stalks to the ovary, whereas Brassicaceae exhibit greater diversity in fruit morphology and dehiscence capabilities (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Franzke et al., 2011</a>). Comparative analyses can be used to elucidate the genomic basis of these differences. The Cleomaceae species we sequenced is <em>T. hassleriana</em>, often referred to as the spider flower, which is widely grown as an ornamental species and used as an educational model (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Marquard and Steinback, 2009</a>). This species was formerly named <em>Cleome hassleriana</em> (often erroneously labeled as <em>Cleome spinosa</em>), but the genus <em>Cleome</em> has undergone recent taxonomic revisions (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Iltis and Cochrane, 2007</a>).</p><p id="p-16">Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae have undergone independent ancient polyploidy events. At least five ancient polyploidy events have occurred in the evolutionary history of <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Bowers et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">Van de Peer et al., 2009</a>), four of which are shared with Cleomaceae: ζ near the origin of seed plants (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Jiao et al., 2011</a>), ε near the origin of angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-41-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Jiao et al., 2011</a>), the ancient hexaploidy At-γ shared by nearly all eudicots (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Jaillon et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">Vekemans et al., 2012</a>), and At-β restricted to part of the order Brassicales as it is lacking from the papaya genome (<a id="xref-ref-58-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Ming et al., 2008</a>). The most extensively studied ancient polyploidy event is the more recent At-α genome duplication (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, 2000</a>; <a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Bowers et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Schnable et al., 2012</a>) and is shared by all Brassicaceae species (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Schranz et al., 2012</a>). The crop genus <em>Brassica</em> has all the ancient polyploidy events in common with <em>Arabidopsis</em> but also has undergone an additional and more recent whole-genome triplication (hexaploidy) event (Br-α) after its split with <em>Arabidopsis</em> around ∼17 million years ago (<a id="xref-ref-99-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>). Limited BAC and transcriptome sequencing revealed that <em>Tarenaya</em> lacked the At-α event and that it underwent an independent ancient genome triplication (Th-α) (<a id="xref-ref-77-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Schranz and Mitchell-Olds, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Barker et al., 2009</a>). Thus, <em>Tareneya</em> provides a unique opportunity to contrast genome evolution from a common ancestor comparing three genomic equivalents in <em>Tarenaya</em>, two in <em>Arabidopsis</em>, and six in <em>Brassica</em>, and furthermore to contrast two independent ancient genome triplications (Th-α versus Br-α). We not only compare these polyploidy events and more recent tandem duplication events, but also show how they contributed to the genes regulating key reproductive traits (<a id="xref-ref-95-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">Van de Peer et al., 2009</a>).</p></div><div class="section results" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing and Integration with Physical Map</h3><p id="p-17">The <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome is relatively small (∼290 Mb: 2n = 20) and within the range of sequenced Brassicaceae species: <em>Schrenkiella parvula</em> (formerly <em>Thellungiella parvula</em>), 140 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Dassanayake et al., 2011</a>); <em>A. thaliana</em>, 157 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Bennett et al., 2003</a>); <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, 207 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Hu et al., 2011</a>); <em>Capsella rubella</em>, 210 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Slotte et al., 2013</a>); <em>Aethionema arabicum</em>, 240 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Haudry et al., 2013</a>); <em>Sisimbrium irio</em>, 262 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-34-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Haudry et al., 2013</a>); <em>Eutrema salsugineum</em> (formerly <em>Thelungiella salsuginea</em>), 314 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-102-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">Wu et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">Yang et al., 2013</a>); <em>Leavenworthia alabamica</em>, 316 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-34-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Haudry et al., 2013</a>); and <em>Brassica rapa</em> 485 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-99-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>). To generate a high-quality draft genome assembly, we used both sequenced paired-end libraries and constructed a Bacterial Artificial Chromosome (BAC) based whole-genome profiling (<a id="xref-def-2-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a>) physical map. We used the Illumina next-generation sequencing platform to generate ∼70.2 Gb (245X genome-depth) raw data of paired-end reads ranging from 90 to 100 bp (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a> online) from seven libraries with various insert sizes (350 to 20 kb). Sequence data were filtered, yielding ∼40 Gb of high-quality sequence (∼139X coverage) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 2</a> online). Sequences were assembled using SOAPdenovo (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Li et al., 2010</a>) (version 2.21) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 3</a> online). We also sequenced and mapped over 4 Gb of transcriptome data to the assembly showing that &gt;94% of the genic regions were covered (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 4</a> online).</p><p id="p-18">The physical map was made using the Keygene <a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> fingerprinting technique (<a id="xref-ref-96-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">van Oeveren et al., 2011</a>). We generated 192,000 BAC-based Illumina sequence tags from 19,200 BACs from two libraries (<em>Eco</em>RI and <em>Mse</em>I) with an average insert size of ∼125 kb (giving a total of 32X genome equivalents) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 5</a> online). We identified 87,617 high-quality and unique <a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> tag sequences that allowed us to uniquely identify 15,567 BACs (with an average of 40.3 tags per BAC). These were used to build a high-stringency map assembly using modified Finger Printed Contigs (FPC) software (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Engler et al., 2003</a>) to generate 786 contigs using data from 9396 BACs (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 6</a> online). We integrated the <a id="xref-def-2-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> physical map scaffolds with the Short Oligonucleotide Analysis Package (SOAP) sequence scaffolds to produce 77 superscaffolds from the integration of 349 sequence scaffolds (integrating between two and 21 scaffolds per superscaffold with an average of 4.5 scaffolds per superscaffold) through Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLAST) mapping of the <a id="xref-def-2-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> anchors (tags). We evaluated the quality of the integration between physical map and superscaffolds by manually checking the ordering and orientation of connected scaffolds by analyzing collinearity relative to <em>A. lyrata</em> (example shown in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 1</a> online), confirming that all <em>Tarenaya</em> superscaffolds have extensive and extended synteny. We further validated our assembly by comparing it with four previously published Sanger-sequenced BACs (<a id="xref-ref-77-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Schranz and Mitchell-Olds, 2006</a>; <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Navarro-Quezada, 2007</a>), revealing nearly identical assemblies (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 2</a> online). The final assembly statistics of the integrated dataset are summarized (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>; see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 7</a> online). With this integration, the N50 was increased by more than 2.6-fold (N50 = 1.26 Mb) due to the merger of most of the de novo assembled scaffolds into superscaffolds.</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/47758/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/47758/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <span class="caption-title">Summary of the genome sequencing, assembly, and annotation.</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Annotation</h3><p id="p-19">Gene annotation was conducted using a pipeline that integrates de novo gene prediction, homology-based alignment, and RNA-seq data. In total, we conducted &gt;4 Gb of RNA-seq, of which 77.4% of reads could be confidently mapped onto the genome (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 8</a> online). To analyze the overall gene expression patterns and to provide basic gene expression information, transcriptomes were generated for several <em>T. hassleriana</em> tissues (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 9</a> online). A principal component analysis showed that stamen, root, and seed profiles separate most, a result similar to the pattern detected in <em>A. thaliana</em> where these three tissue types separate most over the first two components (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 3</a> online) (<a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Schmid et al., 2005</a>).</p><p id="p-20">The prediction of gene models by various techniques was summarized (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 10</a> online), with a large range in the number of predicted genes. To be conservative, we used the models predicted by GLEAN to have a high posterior probability out of the various gene prediction techniques, which resulted in the identification of 28,917 highly supported gene models with an average transcript length of 2216 bp, coding sequence size of 1169 bp, and 5.27 exons per gene, both similar to that observed in <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>B. rapa</em> (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 4</a> online). A total of 92.9% of gene models have a homolog match or conserved motif in at least one of the public protein databases, including Swissprot (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">McMillan and Martin, 2008</a>), 71.1%; the Translated European Molecular Biology Laboratory database (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Boeckmann et al., 2003</a>), 92.5%; InterPro (<a id="xref-ref-105-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">Zdobnov and Apweiler, 2001</a>), 74.9%; the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Kanehisa and Goto, 2000</a>), 55.2%; and Gene Ontology (GO) (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Ashburner et al., 2000</a>), 55.7% (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 11</a> online), and 97.1% are represented among the public Expressed Sequence Tag (EST) collections or de novo Illumina mRNA-Seq data. In addition to protein-coding genes, we also identified 220 microRNA, 862 tRNA, and 685 small nuclear RNA genes in the <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 12</a> online). Orthologous clustering of proteomes predicted for <em>T. hassleriana</em> and three Brassicaceae species (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em>) revealed 15,112 genes in 12,689 families in common (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Figure 1</a>). We found that 20,926 <em>T. hassleriana</em> genes clustered with at least one of the three genomes. Furthermore, 1556 <em>Tarenaya</em>-specific genes in 748 families were identified, most of which were enriched for genes of unknown function and for which 34% have EST supported annotation.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Venn Diagram Illustrating the Shared and Unique Gene Families from T. hassleriana (Cleomaceae), A. thaliana, A. lyrata, and B. rapa (Brassicaceae).In total, we predicted 28,917 well-supported gene models for T. hassleriana, of which 22,482 could be placed into one of the 14,505 gene families. A total of 87.5% of gene families found in Tarenaya were present in all three Brassicaceae species and 7.4% in one or two Brassicaceae species, and only 5% of gene families were unique to Tarenaya with many of these associated genome-specific retrotransposons. Thus, comparative functional and evolutionary analysis of well-characterized Arabidopsis and Brassicaceae genes is feasible using Tarenaya as an outgroup." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Venn Diagram Illustrating the Shared and Unique Gene Families from &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt; (Cleomaceae), &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;A. lyrata&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;B. rapa&lt;/em&gt; (Brassicaceae).In total, we predicted 28,917 well-supported gene models for &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt;, of which 22,482 could be placed into one of the 14,505 gene families. A total of 87.5% of gene families found in &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; were present in all three Brassicaceae species and 7.4% in one or two Brassicaceae species, and only 5% of gene families were unique to &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; with many of these associated genome-specific retrotransposons. Thus, comparative functional and evolutionary analysis of well-characterized &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; and Brassicaceae genes is feasible using &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; as an outgroup.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="331"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 1." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="331"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/47712" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">Venn Diagram Illustrating the Shared and Unique Gene Families from <em>T. hassleriana</em> (Cleomaceae), <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em> (Brassicaceae).</p><p id="p-22">In total, we predicted 28,917 well-supported gene models for <em>T. hassleriana</em>, of which 22,482 could be placed into one of the 14,505 gene families. A total of 87.5% of gene families found in <em>Tarenaya</em> were present in all three Brassicaceae species and 7.4% in one or two Brassicaceae species, and only 5% of gene families were unique to <em>Tarenaya</em> with many of these associated genome-specific retrotransposons. Thus, comparative functional and evolutionary analysis of well-characterized <em>Arabidopsis</em> and Brassicaceae genes is feasible using <em>Tarenaya</em> as an outgroup.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-23">To evaluate the status of our genomic assembly, we compared it to the nine published crucifer genomes (<a id="xref-ref-34-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Haudry et al., 2013</a>) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 13</a> online). Our <em>T. hassleriana</em> assembly has the highest assembled sequence versus expected genome size with an estimated 93% genome size completeness. For comparison, the <em>B. rapa</em> assembly only has 51.6% genome coverage. Our assembly also has the largest scaffold N50 (1.26 Mb) of the Illumina-only sequenced genomes (<em>T. hassleriana</em>, <em>Ae. arabicum</em>, <em>S. irio</em>, <em>Leavenworthia alabamica</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em>). The number of predicted genes is on par with that in the other crucifers and the number of <em>A. thaliana</em> orthologs is slightly lower than average, as expected from an organism outside the Brassicaceae. Eighty-eight percent of ultraconserved core eukaryotic genes (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Parra et al., 2009</a>) were found, suggesting a slightly lower covered gene space. This might be due to the fact that the percentage of transposable elements (<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">TEs</a>) is relatively high, which can cause difficulties assembling regions where <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">TEs</a> and genes are intermixed.</p><p id="p-24">Because of our high genome sequencing coverage, we were able to identify more than 43% of the 293-Mb <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome as being composed of transposons. For comparison, only 31% of the 529 Mb <em>B. rapa</em> genome has been identified as transposons (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 13</a> online). The discrepancy is because of the great difference in the coverage of the assemblies (93% versus 56%). Thus, the percentage of transposons in <em>Brassica</em> is largely underestimated. To examine the contiguity of the genome assembly (causes for gaps between contigs in the scaffolds), we analyzed the distribution of contigs versus transposon long-terminal repeats across the largest 491 scaffolds (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 5</a> online). By doing so, we can demonstrate that regions with a high density of long terminal repeats (LTRs) correspond to smaller contigs in scaffolds and thus generate regions of lower contiguity. Both de novo repeat identification and homology-based methods were applied to predict transposable elements (TEs) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 14</a> online). The majority of repetitive sequences were Class I long-terminal repeat retrotransposons, constituting 36.6% of the genome compared with 27.1% in <em>B. rapa</em>. The overall lower percentages of annotated transposons in <em>Brassica</em> are likely due to its lower genome sequence coverage (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 13</a> online) because the <em>B. rapa</em> genome is nearly 200 Mb larger than <em>Tarenaya</em>. Most of the repeats were located in the intergenic regions.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Analysis of Ancient Polyploidy Events</h3><p id="p-25">The Brassicaceae-Cleomaceae system allowed us to compare genome evolution after several rounds of independent ancient polyploidy (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>). We confirmed that the Cleomaceae polyploidy event (Th-α) occurred independently of, and more recently than, the Brassicaceae-specific duplication event detected in <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>Brassica</em> (At-α). We also detected the nested <em>B. rapa</em> ancient hexaploidy (triplication of the genome) event (Br-α) and show that it is of approximately the same age as Th-α. For all three taxa, we detected the diffuse signal of the older and shared events (At-β, At-γ, ε, and ζ) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2</a>). The Th-α and Br-α events are of approximately the same age and, as discussed below, represent independent ancient hexaploidy events. Using whole genome intragenomic dot plots of <em>Tarenaya</em>, we showed many triplicated blocks (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 6</a> online), and analysis of syntenic depth with QuotaAlign (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Tang et al., 2011</a>) shows that 49.4% of genes are found at 3× coverage (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 15</a> online). To illustrate the homologous relationships and the evolutionary history of triplicated/duplicated segments in Cleomaceae and Brassicaceae, we integrated intra- and intergenomic analyses (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a>). We analyzed synteny relationships both within and between genomes (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 6 to 9</a> online). For <em>T. hassleriana</em>, 86, 83, and 85% of the protein-coding genes were homologous to the genes in the <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em> genomes, respectively (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 16</a> online). By making these comparisons of <em>Tarenaya</em> versus <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em> genomes (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a>), we found significant, 3:2, 3:2, 3:4, 3:5, and 3:6 homologous patterns, respectively, which is consistent with the polyploid history of the species. To illustrate this pattern, we highlighted two ancestral blocks (A1 and A2) (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figure 3</a>, two small insets). Note that the three <em>Tarenaya</em> blocks show almost perfect collinearity, whereas one of two <em>Arabidopsis</em> regions is broken across two chromosomes, suggesting a Brassicaceae-specific rearrangement(s) after At-α. Since synteny analysis has been extensively performed within Brassicaceae, we also show our results with the collinear blocks color-coded according to the current Brassicaceae conventions (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Schranz et al., 2006</a>) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 10</a> online).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Relative Timing of the Polyploidy Events and Lineage Splitting Based on Divergence of Fourfold Degenerate Sites (4DTv) for Duplicated Genes within A. thaliana, B. rapa, and T. hassleriana and Orthologous Genes between A. thaliana and T. hassleriana.All plots detect broad overlapping peaks between 0.5 and 1.0, representing shared older polyploidy events (At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B3;, &#x3B5;, and &#x3B6;). The divergence of the Brassicaceae-Cleomaceae lineages is seen by the differentiation of Arabidopsis and Tarenaya homologs at the peak centered at &#x223C;0.35 (highlighted by red star). The divergence between paralogs from the At-&#x3B1; duplication event occurred slightly after the lineage splitting and is detected by the peaks centered at &#x223C;0.3 for both Arabidopsis and Brassica. The At-&#x3B1; peak is lacking from Tarenaya, proving At-&#x3B1; is Brassicaceae specific. Nearly overlapping distributions between 0.15 and 0.25 were detected for Brassica and Tarenaya, representing the independent Br-&#x3B1; and Th-&#x3B1; ancient hexaploidy events, respectively." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Relative Timing of the Polyploidy Events and Lineage Splitting Based on Divergence of Fourfold Degenerate Sites (4DTv) for Duplicated Genes within &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;B. rapa&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt; and Orthologous Genes between &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt;.All plots detect broad overlapping peaks between 0.5 and 1.0, representing shared older polyploidy events (At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B3;, &#x3B5;, and &#x3B6;). The color-coded outer rings of homology relative to Tarenaya show a complex pattern due to the independent polyploidy events between families. The two small insets illustrate examples of the three Tarenaya to two Arabidopsis to six Brassica genome equivalents due to ancient polyploidy events." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Homologous Genome Blocks within and between Genomes for Cleomaceae and Brassicaceae.The largest 20 (plus additional two smaller scaffolds) color-coded superscaffolds of &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt; are taken as the reference, such that any region homologous to the 22 scaffolds is colored accordingly.
