A, Self-alignment of <em>Tarenaya</em> superscaffolds, with the inner circle showing links of syntenic blocks. Over 47% of the genome is found in three copies, supporting the conclusion that it experienced an ancient hexaploidy event (Th-α = triplication). Rings within a genome (inner gray bars) and blocks homologous to <em>Tarenaya</em> (outer color-coded bars) for completed Brassicaceae genomes: B, <em>A. thaliana</em>; C, <em>A. lyrata</em>; and D, <em>B. rapa</em>. The inner gray bars show a clear pattern related to the ancient polyploidy events of the Brassicaceae (At-α = duplication) and nested Brassica-specific lineage (Br-α = triplication). The color-coded outer rings of homology relative to <em>Tarenaya</em> show a complex pattern due to the independent polyploidy events between families. The two small insets illustrate examples of the three <em>Tarenaya</em> to two <em>Arabidopsis</em> to six <em>Brassica</em> genome equivalents due to ancient polyploidy events.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-30">We inferred the putative “A ancestor” (pre-At-α) shared by <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em>, the “B ancestor” of <em>B. rapa</em> (pre-Br-α but post-At-α ancestral genome state), and the “T ancestor” of <em>T. hassleriana</em> (pre-Th-α ancestral genome state) (see Methods). We compared our identified homologous replicated blocks within and between genomes in Brassicaceae species and <em>T. hassleriana</em> with the results of an earlier analysis of conserved At-α blocks (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Blanc et al., 2003</a>; <a id="xref-ref-89-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">Thomas et al., 2006</a>). First, we reconstructed our version of the pre-At-α ancestor (A ancestor) of <em>A. thaliana</em> (version TAIR9), which resulted in 64 ancestral regions involving 19,976 protein-coding genes (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 15</a> online). The corresponding relationships to the blocks identified by Wolfe and colleagues (<a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Blanc et al., 2003</a>) (that we refer to as “Wolfe blocks”) are illustrated in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 11</a> online. We used the same method on the minimized genomes of <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, and <em>T. hassleriana</em> and generated 61, 71, and 87 conserved ancestral blocks covering 24,373; 25,646; and 20,680 protein-coding genes, respectively, to represent the postulated A, B, and T ancestors (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 12 to 14</a> online). A table listing all genes included in each block for each genome is provided in the supplemental materials (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 1</a> online). A comparison of the reconstructed T and A ancestor genomes revealed a 1:1 relationship, supporting our conclusion that the ancestral genome of the Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae was conserved before the independent duplication (At-α) and triplication (Th- α) events, respectively. Since <em>B. rapa</em> underwent a nested and specific triplication following the At-α, we see a 1:2 pattern when we compare the inferred T to B ancestors. A comparison of conserved ancestral genomic blocks across species is shown in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 15</a> online. We then traced the extent of gene retention and fractionation in homologous blocks after polyploidy events. We partitioned the two subgenomes of <em>Arabidopsis</em>, three subgenomes of Brassica, and three subgenomes of <em>T. hassleriana</em>, respectively, by comparing them with the reference A ancestor of <em>A. lyrata</em> (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 16 to 19</a> online). These improvements to understanding genome evolution after independent ancient polyploidy events of Brassicaceae species will facilitate synteny analyses in distant crop species.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Analysis of Type II MADS Box Genes</h3><p id="p-31">The development of the four floral organ types and later the fruits is regulated by Type II MINICHROMOSOME MAINTENANCE1, AGAMOUS, DEFICIENS and SERUM RESPONSE FACTOR (MADS) domain proteins (<a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Smaczniak et al., 2012</a>) as described by the ABCDE model (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">Theissen, 2001</a>). The types of MADS box genes that regulate development are remarkably well conserved across eudicots, with the molecular mechanisms of their action extensively studied in <em>Arabidopsis</em>. We found that the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome contains representatives of all the major Type II MADS box genes described in <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>Brassica</em> (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 20</a> online). We concentrated on the retention of the MADS box genes derived from At-α, Br-α, and Th-α and compared this with the polyploid origins of additional duplicates (At-β, At-γ, ε, T [tomato] [<a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>], and Pt-α [poplar] [<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">Tuskan et al., 2006</a>]) (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>). Theoretically, the At-α, Br-α, and Th-α events should have given rise to two <em>Arabidopsis</em>, six <em>Brassica</em>, and three <em>Tarenaya</em> gene copies (syntelogs) from a single ancestral gene. Of the 11 MADS box gene clades involved in floral, fruit, and inflorescence development that were likely present in the most recent common ancestor of Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae shown in <a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>, we found that only three duplicate pairs are in fact maintained in <em>Arabidopsis</em> due to At-α: <em>APETALA1</em> (<em>AP1</em>)/<em>CAULIFLOWER</em> (<em>CAL</em>) (A-function), <em>SHATTERPROOF1</em> (<em>SHP1</em>)/<em>SHP2</em> (D-function), and <em>SEPALLATA1</em> (<em>SEP1</em>)/<em>SEP2</em> (E-function). Thus, <em>Arabidopsis</em> has only three of 11 possible replicates (27.3% syntelog retention). This implies that during early Brassicaceae evolution (before the split of <em>Arabidopsis</em>-<em>Brassica</em>), there were 14 gene lineages. From these 14 lineages then there would be 42 possible syntelogs in <em>Brassica</em> due to Br-α triplication, of which 28 copies are considered as additional syntelogs. We find a remarkable 19 of these additional gene copies (67.8% syntelog retention). This includes all three possible copies maintained for the following seven Brassica gene families: <em>SEP4</em>, <em>SEP3</em>, <em>AGAMOUS-LIKE79</em> (<em>AGL79</em>), <em>FRUITFULL</em>, <em>AP1</em>, <em>PISTILLATA</em> (<em>PI</em>), and <em>SHP1</em>. The only two genes to return to single copy in <em>Brassica</em> after Br-α are <em>CAL</em> and <em>SHP2</em>. When we also consider the At-α gene retention, then a maximum of four of six gene copies are found in the <em>SEP1/2</em> clade, <em>AP1/CAL</em> clade, and the <em>SHP1/2</em> clade. From the 11 ancestral loci, 33 syntelogs would be expected in <em>Tarenaya</em> due to the Th-α triplication, with 22 possible additional syntelogs. However, we only recovered six (27.3% syntelog retention) with no cases where all three possible copies are maintained (we do not count the additional tandem duplicate of Th<em>-AP3</em> here, which is discussed below). Thus, we find more than double the syntelog retention in <em>Brassica</em> than in <em>Tarenaya</em>, despite the fact that both are ancient hexaploids of approximately the same age. The greatest differential in gene copy retention between <em>Brassica</em> and <em>Tarenaya</em> is for the <em>AGL79</em> clade (3 versus 1) and the <em>SHP1/2</em> clade (4 versus 1). The <em>SHATTERPROOF</em> genes in Brassicaceae regulate various traits during carpel and fruit formation (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Colombo et al., 2010</a>). The single-copy nature of the <em>SHP</em> homolog in <em>Tarenaya</em> is thus notable, since this is the only gene that is duplicated in <em>Arabidopsis</em> due to At-α but has returned to single copy in <em>Tarenaya</em> (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 21</a> online). In Cleomaceae, fruit morphology is less diverse than in Brassicaceae, and we hypothesize that the retention of <em>SHP</em> genes plays an important role in the morphological variability of Brassicaceae.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic Tree of Type-II MADS Box Transcription Factor Genes Involved in Floral Organ Specification.The alignment used to create this tree is available in the online materials (see Supplemental Data Set 2 online). The major floral MADS box genes cluster into five groups corresponding to the five main functional types (AP1-like genes, shown in yellow; AP3/PI-like genes, B-type shown in blue; AG-like genes shown in gray; STK-like genes shown in red; and SEP-like genes in green) according to the ABC(DE) model of floral development. Species included are A. thaliana (At), grape (Vitis vinifera, Vv), tomato (Solanum lycopersicum, Sl), poplar (Populus trichocarpa, Pt), B. rapa (Br), and T. hassleriana (Th). Tarenaya genes are indicated in red. The colored squares (duplication events) and circles (triplication events) placed on nodes represent gene lineage expansion(s) that can be associated with particular ancient polyploid events: Th-&#x3B1;, At-&#x3B1;, Br-&#x3B1;, At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B3;, &#x3B5;, T (identified by tomato genome sequencing), and Pt-&#x3B1; (identified by poplar genome sequencing). Type-II MADS box genes are often retained after ancient polyploidy events. From the tree above, we calculated a 27.3% syntelog (homolog generated by a polyploidy event) retention after At-&#x3B1;, 27.3% syntelog retention after Th-&#x3B1;, and a much higher (67.8%) syntelog retention after Br-&#x3B1;, despite the fact that Th-&#x3B1; and Br-&#x3B1; triplications are of approximately the same age. The Tarenaya B-class genes show unusual patterns in that the AP3 homologs (Ch02920 and Th02921) represent a recent tandem duplication, which is rare for floral MADS box genes, and there are two copies of PI homologs that are likely due to At-&#x3B2; with one lineage being lost in Brassicaceae. Shown is a maximum likelihood tree with 1000 replicate bootstrap values, of which the branches with a bootstrap value of &gt;80 are presented, visualized topology only." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic Tree of Type-II MADS Box Transcription Factor Genes Involved in Floral Organ Specification.The alignment used to create this tree is available in the online materials (see Supplemental Data Set 2 online). The major floral MADS box genes cluster into five groups corresponding to the five main functional types (AP1-like genes, shown in yellow; AP3/PI-like genes, B-type shown in blue; AG-like genes shown in gray; STK-like genes shown in red; and SEP-like genes in green) according to the ABC(DE) model of floral development. Species included are &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; (At), grape (&lt;em&gt;Vitis vinifera&lt;/em&gt;, Vv), tomato (&lt;em&gt;Solanum lycopersicum&lt;/em&gt;, Sl), poplar (&lt;em&gt;Populus trichocarpa&lt;/em&gt;, Pt), &lt;em&gt;B. rapa&lt;/em&gt; (Br), and &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt; (Th). &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; genes are indicated in red. The colored squares (duplication events) and circles (triplication events) placed on nodes represent gene lineage expansion(s) that can be associated with particular ancient polyploid events: Th-&#x3B1;, At-&#x3B1;, Br-&#x3B1;, At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B3;, &#x3B5;, T (identified by tomato genome sequencing), and Pt-&#x3B1; (identified by poplar genome sequencing). Type-II MADS box genes are often retained after ancient polyploidy events. From the tree above, we calculated a 27.3% syntelog (homolog generated by a polyploidy event) retention after At-&#x3B1;, 27.3% syntelog retention after Th-&#x3B1;, and a much higher (67.8%) syntelog retention after Br-&#x3B1;, despite the fact that Th-&#x3B1; and Br-&#x3B1; triplications are of approximately the same age. The &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; B-class genes show unusual patterns in that the &lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; homologs (Ch02920 and Th02921) represent a recent tandem duplication, which is rare for floral MADS box genes, and there are two copies of &lt;em&gt;PI&lt;/em&gt; homologs that are likely due to At-&#x3B2; with one lineage being lost in Brassicaceae. Shown is a maximum likelihood tree with 1000 replicate bootstrap values, of which the branches with a bootstrap value of &gt;80 are presented, visualized topology only.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="402"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 4." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="402"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/47812" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 4.</span> <p id="p-32" class="first-child">Phylogenetic Tree of Type-II MADS Box Transcription Factor Genes Involved in Floral Organ Specification.</p><p id="p-33">The alignment used to create this tree is available in the online materials (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Data Set 2</a> online). The major floral MADS box genes cluster into five groups corresponding to the five main functional types (AP1-like genes, shown in yellow; AP3/PI-like genes, B-type shown in blue; AG-like genes shown in gray; STK-like genes shown in red; and SEP-like genes in green) according to the ABC(DE) model of floral development. Species included are <em>A. thaliana</em> (At), grape (<em>Vitis vinifera</em>, Vv), tomato (<em>Solanum lycopersicum</em>, Sl), poplar (<em>Populus trichocarpa</em>, Pt), <em>B. rapa</em> (Br), and <em>T. hassleriana</em> (Th). <em>Tarenaya</em> genes are indicated in red. The colored squares (duplication events) and circles (triplication events) placed on nodes represent gene lineage expansion(s) that can be associated with particular ancient polyploid events: Th-α, At-α, Br-α, At-β, At-γ, ε, T (identified by tomato genome sequencing), and Pt-α (identified by poplar genome sequencing). Type-II MADS box genes are often retained after ancient polyploidy events. From the tree above, we calculated a 27.3% syntelog (homolog generated by a polyploidy event) retention after At-α, 27.3% syntelog retention after Th-α, and a much higher (67.8%) syntelog retention after Br-α, despite the fact that Th-α and Br-α triplications are of approximately the same age. The <em>Tarenaya</em> B-class genes show unusual patterns in that the <em>AP3</em> homologs (Ch02920 and Th02921) represent a recent tandem duplication, which is rare for floral MADS box genes, and there are two copies of <em>PI</em> homologs that are likely due to At-β with one lineage being lost in Brassicaceae. Shown is a maximum likelihood tree with 1000 replicate bootstrap values, of which the branches with a bootstrap value of &gt;80 are presented, visualized topology only.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Comparative Collinearity Analysis of Floral Developmental Regulators</h3><p id="p-34">To assess the contributions of ancient polyploidy and tandem gene duplications to floral regulatory gene diversification, we conducted a more detailed analysis of gene synteny and expression patterns. Almost all floral MADS box genes show conserved synteny between Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae. For example, the A-class genes show stable duplicate retention; the loci containing the <em>AP1</em> and <em>CAL</em> homologs in <em>Tarenaya</em>, <em>Arabidopsis</em>, and <em>Brassica</em> are syntenic to one another with little evidence of local rearrangements (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 22</a> online).</p><p id="p-35">Compared with other MADS box genes, the B-class (<em>PI</em> and <em>AP3</em>) genomic regions show a more dynamic pattern (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>). The split between <em>PI</em> and <em>AP3</em> is an old duplication due to the angiosperm ε polyploidy event (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figure 4</a>), with almost no detectable collinearity between these regions (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>). Comparison of B-class <em>Tarenaya</em> genomic regions with Brassicaceae allowed us to detect two B-class duplication events: A recent <em>Tarenaya</em> tandem duplication of <em>AP3</em> (Th02920 and Th02921) and an older <em>PI</em> duplication, likely due to At-β, which has been lost from Brassicaceae but is still retained in <em>Tarenaya</em> (Th17298) (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>; see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 23 and 24</a> online).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Collinearity Analysis of B-Class Type II MADS Box Gene (AP3 and PI) Homologs Reveals Unusual Patterns of Gene Loss, Lineage-Specific Transpositions, and Local Tandem Duplications.The placement of ancient polyploid events giving rise to gene duplicates is shown on appropriate nodes (At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B1;, Br-&#x3B1;, and Th-&#x3B1;). For the AP3 group genes in Brassicaceae (shown by red bars), there is only a single locus retained in A. thaliana and A. lyrata and two retained Brassica syntelogs derived from Br-&#x3B1;. Collinear homoeologous regions derived from At-&#x3B1; are detectable in Brassicaceae genomes; however, the AP3 syntelogs were lost (regions highlighted in red boxes). T. hassleriana has an unusual tandem duplication of AP3 genes in one of two homoeologous regions derived from Th-&#x3B1;. The AP3 genes and the neighboring Forkhead gene (EMB1967) are the only genes syntenic to the AP3 Brassicaceae region (see Supplemental Figure 25 online). The Cleomaceae AP3 region is syntenic with AP3 regions of all other eudicot genomes analyzed (see Supplemental Figure 23 online). Thus, we conclude that there was a lineage-specific transposition of AP3 and the neighboring Forkhead locus in the Brassicaceae. There is only a single copy of PI genes (red bars) in A. thaliana and A. lyrata and all three Br-&#x3B1; derived syntelogs in Brassicaceae. There is no detectable homoeologous region in Brassicaceae derived from At-&#x3B1;. In Tarenaya, we detected one syntenic PI gene and region to the Brassicaceae, but also a second region that is syntenic to other eudicots (see Supplemental Figure 24 online). We conclude that these two Tarenaya PI genes were generated due to the At-&#x3B2; ancient duplication event with the subsequent transposition of one locus into the region collinear between Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae and loss of the nontransposed locus from only the Brassicaceae lineage. The differences in genomic context and gene expression (see Supplemental Figure 27 online) may contribute to shifts in floral morphology and symmetry between families." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Collinearity Analysis of B-Class Type II MADS Box Gene (&lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;PI&lt;/em&gt;) Homologs Reveals Unusual Patterns of Gene Loss, Lineage-Specific Transpositions, and Local Tandem Duplications.The placement of ancient polyploid events giving rise to gene duplicates is shown on appropriate nodes (At-&#x3B2;, At-&#x3B1;, Br-&#x3B1;, and Th-&#x3B1;). For the &lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; group genes in Brassicaceae (shown by red bars), there is only a single locus retained in &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. lyrata&lt;/em&gt; and two retained Brassica syntelogs derived from Br-&#x3B1;. Collinear homoeologous regions derived from At-&#x3B1; are detectable in Brassicaceae genomes; however, the &lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; syntelogs were lost (regions highlighted in red boxes). &lt;em&gt;T. hassleriana&lt;/em&gt; has an unusual tandem duplication of &lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; genes in one of two homoeologous regions derived from Th-&#x3B1;. The &lt;em&gt;AP3&lt;/em&gt; genes and the neighboring Forkhead gene (&lt;em&gt;EMB1967&lt;/em&gt;) are the only genes syntenic to the AP3 Brassicaceae region (see Supplemental Figure 25 online). The Cleomaceae AP3 region is syntenic with AP3 regions of all other eudicot genomes analyzed (see Supplemental Figure 23 online). Thus, we conclude that there was a lineage-specific transposition of AP3 and the neighboring Forkhead locus in the Brassicaceae. There is only a single copy of PI genes (red bars) in &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. lyrata&lt;/em&gt; and all three Br-&#x3B1; derived syntelogs in Brassicaceae. There is no detectable homoeologous region in Brassicaceae derived from At-&#x3B1;. In &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt;, we detected one syntenic &lt;em&gt;PI&lt;/em&gt; gene and region to the Brassicaceae, but also a second region that is syntenic to other eudicots (see Supplemental Figure 24 online). We conclude that these two &lt;em&gt;Tarenaya&lt;/em&gt; &lt;em&gt;PI&lt;/em&gt; genes were generated due to the At-&#x3B2; ancient duplication event with the subsequent transposition of one locus into the region collinear between Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae and loss of the nontransposed locus from only the Brassicaceae lineage. The differences in genomic context and gene expression (see Supplemental Figure 27 online) may contribute to shifts in floral morphology and symmetry between families.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="339"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 5." src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F5.medium.gif" width="440" height="339"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/47709" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 5.</span> <p id="p-36" class="first-child">Collinearity Analysis of B-Class Type II MADS Box Gene (<em>AP3</em> and <em>PI</em>) Homologs Reveals Unusual Patterns of Gene Loss, Lineage-Specific Transpositions, and Local Tandem Duplications.</p><p id="p-37">The placement of ancient polyploid events giving rise to gene duplicates is shown on appropriate nodes (At-β, At-α, Br-α, and Th-α). For the <em>AP3</em> group genes in Brassicaceae (shown by red bars), there is only a single locus retained in <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> and two retained Brassica syntelogs derived from Br-α. Collinear homoeologous regions derived from At-α are detectable in Brassicaceae genomes; however, the <em>AP3</em> syntelogs were lost (regions highlighted in red boxes). <em>T. hassleriana</em> has an unusual tandem duplication of <em>AP3</em> genes in one of two homoeologous regions derived from Th-α. The <em>AP3</em> genes and the neighboring Forkhead gene (<em>EMB1967</em>) are the only genes syntenic to the AP3 Brassicaceae region (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 25</a> online). The Cleomaceae AP3 region is syntenic with AP3 regions of all other eudicot genomes analyzed (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 23</a> online). Thus, we conclude that there was a lineage-specific transposition of AP3 and the neighboring Forkhead locus in the Brassicaceae. There is only a single copy of PI genes (red bars) in <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> and all three Br-α derived syntelogs in Brassicaceae. There is no detectable homoeologous region in Brassicaceae derived from At-α. In <em>Tarenaya</em>, we detected one syntenic <em>PI</em> gene and region to the Brassicaceae, but also a second region that is syntenic to other eudicots (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 24</a> online). We conclude that these two <em>Tarenaya</em> <em>PI</em> genes were generated due to the At-β ancient duplication event with the subsequent transposition of one locus into the region collinear between Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae and loss of the nontransposed locus from only the Brassicaceae lineage. The differences in genomic context and gene expression (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 27</a> online) may contribute to shifts in floral morphology and symmetry between families.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-38">Strikingly, we also detected two gene transposition events: a Brassicaceae-specific AP3 transposition and a shared transposition event of one <em>PI</em> gene before the split of Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Figure 5</a>). The Brassicaceae-specific AP3 transposition event also involved the flanking EMBRYO DEFECTIVE1967 (EMB1967) gene containing two conserved domains: the N-terminal region of microspherule protein (MCRS_N) and Forkhead-associated (FHA). Tarenaya AP3 is similarly flanked by a Forkhead-associated protein (Th02919) that has its highest match to EMB1967; however, the orientation of AP3 and the Forkhead genes is inverted between species (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 25</a> online) and is also detected in a distantly related Cleome species. In general, B-class genes are functionally highly conserved across angiosperms, whereas A-class gene function appears to be less conserved (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Litt and Kramer, 2010</a>). However, we have shown that it is in fact the B-class genes that have undergone transposition events.</p><p id="p-39">Members of the TEOSINTE BRANCHED1, CYCLOIDEA, and PCF (TCP) gene family play an important role in the transition from polysymmetric to monosymmetric flowers (reviewed in <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Busch and Zachgo, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Jabbour et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Rosin and Kramer, 2009</a>), including monosymmetric Brassicaceae (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">Busch and Zachgo, 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Busch et al., 2012</a>). Cleomaceae floral morphology, especially in petal and stamen position, numbers, and asymmetry, is quite variable. However, the role of Tarenaya TCP homologs in monosymmetry has not yet been fully characterized. We find a pattern of conservation of genomic collinearity around the TCP1 locus between species (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 26</a> online). <em>Arabidopsis</em> contains only a single TCP1 locus, as does <em>A. lyrata</em>. Due to Br-α, Brassica has three syntenic copies of TCP1. We also can detect the syntenic regions in Brassicaceae species due to At-α (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 26</a> online) but find no At-α derived homologs of TCP genes, suggesting the loss of a TCP1 syntelog occurred early in Brassicaceae evolution. In Tarenaya, we find three genomic regions derived from Th-α, with two copies of TCP1 intact (Th21666 and Th24587) (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 26</a> online). The correlation between multiple copies of TCP members and monosymmetry has been noted across angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-72-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Rosin and Kramer, 2009</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Expression of A- and B-Class Homolog Genes</h3><p id="p-40">The expression of <em>T. hassleriana</em> homologs of <em>A. thaliana</em> major floral regulators was also analyzed with quantitative RT-PCR (<a id="xref-def-4-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">qRT-PCR</a>). The two putative <em>T. hassleriana</em> homologs of <em>CAL</em>/<em>AP1</em> (Th<em>-CAL/AP1-1</em> and Th<em>-CAL/AP1-2</em>) show similar expression in all bud stages, but Th<em>-CAL/AP1-1</em> with only half of the transcript abundance of Th<em>-CAL/AP1-2</em>. Th<em>-CAL/AP1-1</em> shows highest expression in sepals and ∼10 times lower expression in petals. Expression of neither homolog was detectable in stamens, petals, gynoecia, capsules, roots, or leaves. Th<em>-PI-1</em>, the homolog of <em>PI</em> in <em>A. thaliana</em>, is collinear with the Brassicaceae gene order and is expressed mainly in petals and stamens, with less expression in younger and higher expression in older stages (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 27</a> online). The second <em>PI</em> homolog of <em>T. hassleriana</em>, Th<em>-PI-2</em>, is expressed at a much lower rate than Th<em>-PI-1</em>, ranging from around 50% of the Th<em>-PI-1</em> expression in stamens to only 10% of the Th<em>-PI-1</em> expression in late bud states.</p><p id="p-41">The second two putative floral homeotic B-function genes, Th<em>-AP3-1</em> and Th<em>-AP3-2</em>, are highly similar in coding, 3′, and 5′ untranslated region sequence, and both are homologous to the <em>AP3</em> gene in <em>A. thaliana</em>. Th<em>-AP3-1</em> is expressed throughout the observed stages of bud development. In petals and stamens at anthesis, it is the most highly expressed gene of the MADS box genes analyzed (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 27</a> online). In petals, it is expressed at∼200% and in stamens around 400% higher level than Th<em>-CAL</em>, Th<em>-PI-1</em>, and Th<em>-AP3-2</em>. Expression of Th<em>-AP3-2</em> in buds is around one-third lower than that of Th-<em>AP3-1</em>, and differential expression between both genes is detected in petals and stamens. While Th<em>-AP3-1</em> shows higher expression in stamens than in petals, Th<em>-AP3-2</em> has stronger expression in petals than in stamens (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 27</a> online). The divergence in expression of the B-class genes, along with the aforementioned gene transpositions, is indicative of the likely role in B-class gene functional differentiation and the regulation of the different floral morphologies between families. We acknowledge that these comparative analyses of <em>Tarenaya</em> to Brassicaceae are based on the analysis of the draft genome sequence of a single domesticated accession of one species; thus, future comparative analyses to other Cleomaceae species is needed for validation.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Evolution of the Brassicaceae <a id="xref-def-1-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> Locus</h3><p id="p-42">Many Brassicaceae species possess a pollen-pistil recognition system that confers <a id="xref-def-1-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> through the rejection of self-pollen (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Boyes et al., 1997</a>). This system is based on the interaction of the stigmatically expressed S-receptor kinase (encoded by <em>SRK</em>) with a small polymorphic peptide that is coded by the S-locus Cys-rich protein (<em>SCR</em>) gene, both located on the so-called S-locus of the genome. Many <em>SCR</em> alleles are likely derived from (partial) duplication and/or gene conversion from <em>SRK</em> alleles (<a id="xref-ref-45-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Koornneef and Meinke, 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Guo et al., 2011</a>). The cysteine-rich protein kinase (<em>CRK</em>) genes and <em>ARK</em> genes that belong to the S-locus are part of a larger family of receptor-like protein-kinases (<em>RLK</em>) genes, which have been shown to be mostly involved in oxidative stress and pathogen response (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Chen et al., 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">Wrzaczek et al., 2010</a>). It should be noted that most ecotypes of <em>A. thaliana</em> are self-compatible due to pseudogenization of the <em>SRK</em> and/or <em>SCR</em> genes, a pattern seen in other self-compatible crucifers, but still an exception among Brassicaceae (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Nasrallah et al., 2004</a>). <em>T. hassleriana</em> does not possess a <a id="xref-def-1-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> system, but it still contains an S-like locus that contains functional genes, and it is likely that this part of the genome is close to the ancestral state of this locus for Brassicaceae.</p><p id="p-43"><em>SRK</em> and <em>ARK</em> on the S-locus are characterized by specific variations on the following protein domain compositions: B_lectin (B), S_locus_glyco (S), PAN-2 (Pa), Pkinase_Tyr (Pk), and Duf3403 (D1) and/or DUF3660 (D2) (<a id="xref-ref-106-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-106">Zhang et al., 2011</a>). Using the Pfam database (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Punta et al., 2012</a>), we found that the S locus region in <em>C. rubella</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>A. thaliana</em> as well as the homologous region in <em>T. hassleriana</em> mostly contains <em>SRK</em> genes with a B-S-Pa-D1-Pk-D2 protein domain structure, followed by the B-S-Pa-Pk-D1/2 protein domain structure, which is more common across all SRK families (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 6</a>; see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 28</a> online). Three syntenic regions containing most of the genes of the S-locus were found in <em>Tarenaya</em>. One of these contained a gene with the exact B-S-Pa-Pk-D1/2 protein domain structure: Th11131. Our analysis of domain structure further found that another gene, Th22785, had a B-S-Pa-Pk protein domain structure, an architecture shared with many angiosperm genes but not with <a id="xref-def-1-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a>-specific SRK alleles. We have also found two homologs of the <a id="xref-def-1-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a>-modifier gene, <em>Pub8</em>, in two of the three <em>Tarenaya</em> syntenic regions. One of the homologs, Th22784, is adjacent to the Th22785 locus. The other homolog, Th25331, is contained in the syntenic region that completely lacks any S_locus_glyco Pfam-containing proteins. Interestingly, we do not find a <em>Pub8</em> homolog in close proximity to Th11131. However, based on the alignment of all three regions, we can assume that the single-copy ancestral region contained homologs to <em>Pub8</em>, <em>ARK3</em>, and <em>B120</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 6</a>). To ensure that syntenic genes that were not found were not due to gaps in our assembly coverage, we manually confirmed that all relevant regions had no large gaps. In <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 17</a> online, a summary of the coverage in these regions can be found. No large gaps were present except for one 2.5-kb gap in the region around Th22784. Based on the syntenic order of S-locus genes in other species, it is extremely unlikely that a gene is present in this gap, let alone any important candidate gene for this region.</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Synteny and Protein Domain Analysis of the Brassicaceae SI-Like Regions with Cleomaceae and Inference of the Ancestral Genomic Region.All regions presented here are drawn in more detail (including genetic coordinates) in Supplemental Figure 28 online. Genes are marked by block arrows and color coded according to their protein domain composition as listed in the legend. In the case of pseudogenes, the block arrows have a dashed border. To find protein domain composition in pseudogenes, the longest open reading frame was translated in silico (see Methods). Gene orientation is shown by block arrows pointing left for gene orientation toward the 5&#x2032; end and pointing right for gene orientation toward the 3&#x2032;end. The At-&#x3B1; duplication and the Br-&#x3B1; and Th-&#x3B1; triplications have been marked on the tree with an orange box (At-&#x3B1;) and yellow and purple circles (Br-&#x3B1; and Th-&#x3B1;, respectively). Each branch corresponds to a subgenome resulting from such a polyploid event. Theoretically, B. rapa should have six subgenomes, but only the regions showing synteny are listed here for clarity. The bottom branch represents a hypothetical layout of this genome region in the common ancestor of these species before the At-&#x3B1;, Br-&#x3B1;, and Th-&#x3B1; polyploid events. From our results, we conclude that an ARK3-like gene underwent a Brassicaceae-specific tandem gene duplication generating the key SI receptor SRK." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-348360501" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Synteny and Protein Domain Analysis of the Brassicaceae SI-Like Regions with Cleomaceae and Inference of the Ancestral Genomic Region.All regions presented here are drawn in more detail (including genetic coordinates) in Supplemental Figure 28 online. src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F6.medium.gif" width="440" href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantcell.org/content/plantcell/25/8/2813/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/47752" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 6.</span> <p id="p-44" class="first-child">Synteny and Protein Domain Analysis of the Brassicaceae <a id="xref-def-1-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a>-Like Regions with Cleomaceae and Inference of the Ancestral Genomic Region.</p><p id="p-45">All regions presented here are drawn in more detail (including genetic coordinates) in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figure 28</a> online. Genes are marked by block arrows and color coded according to their protein domain composition as listed in the legend. In the case of pseudogenes, the block arrows have a dashed border. To find protein domain composition in pseudogenes, the longest open reading frame was translated in silico (see Methods). Gene orientation is shown by block arrows pointing left for gene orientation toward the 5′ end and pointing right for gene orientation toward the 3′end. The At-α duplication and the Br-α and Th-α triplications have been marked on the tree with an orange box (At-α) and yellow and purple circles (Br-α and Th-α, respectively). Each branch corresponds to a subgenome resulting from such a polyploid event. Theoretically, <em>B. rapa</em> should have six subgenomes, but only the regions showing synteny are listed here for clarity. The bottom branch represents a hypothetical layout of this genome region in the common ancestor of these species before the At-α, Br-α, and Th-α polyploid events. From our results, we conclude that an <em>ARK3</em>-like gene underwent a Brassicaceae-specific tandem gene duplication generating the key <a id="xref-def-1-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> receptor <em>SRK</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-46">We conclude that the syntenic <em>Tarenaya</em> gene Th11131, which is most closely related to <em>ARK3</em> with which it shares protein architecture, is similar to the ancestral version of <a id="xref-def-1-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> locus genes in Brassicaceae. We hypothesize that the origin of the S-locus is due to a local rearrangement/duplication of an <em>ARK3</em>-like gene and subsequent expansion and diversification of the S-locus. Since <em>ARK3</em> in <em>Arabidopsis</em> is not expressed in the stigma, the regulatory domains needed for tissue-specific expression may potentially be derived from the concurrent duplication of elements from neighboring genes.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-10"><h2 class="">DISCUSSION</h2><p id="p-47">The completed genome of <em>A. thaliana</em> was a major milestone in plant biology and has provided a key tool for understanding plant gene function and genome organization (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, 2000</a>). Subsequently, there has been a great effort and interest to sequence other crucifer species to leverage the knowledge gained from <em>Arabidopsis</em> to other species in a comparative context. To date, there are more completed crucifer genomes (including the crop <em>B. rapa</em>; <a id="xref-ref-99-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>) than any other eudicot plant family, and ambitious plans exist to sequence many more (such as the Brassica Map Alignment Project; <a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">Pires et al., 2013</a>). We sequenced <em>T. hassleriana</em>, a phylogenetic outgroup of the Brassicaceae sister family, the Cleomaceae. We have shown that the vast majority of genes in <em>Tarenaya</em> have clear homologs within Brassicaceae. We provide several examples of how this sister-group genome can be used to elucidate patterns of gene, genome, and trait evolution within the Brassicaceae. Specifically, we focused on independent ancient polyploidy events, floral MADS box, and <a id="xref-def-1-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> gene evolution. The genome of <em>T. hassleriana</em> will facilitate future research into the evolutionary and functional history of <em>Arabidopsis</em> genes and pathways.</p><p id="p-48">While it has long been known that there are numerous recent polyploid plants (<a id="xref-ref-41-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Jiao et al., 2011</a>), with the arrival of the genomics era, it has become clear that there also is extensive evidence for ancient polyploidy across the tree of life (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Kasahara, 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-41-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Jiao et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Murat et al., 2012</a>). Most ancient plant polyploid events that have been identified are ancient tetraploidy events (duplications such as At-α), but there are at least four published genome analyses of ancient plant hexaploidy (triplication events): at the base of the eudicots (At-γ) (<a id="xref-ref-40-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Jaillon et al., 2007</a>), in tomato (T) (2012), in Brassica (Br-α) (<a id="xref-ref-99-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">Wang et al., 2011</a>), and this report of the ancient genome triplication in the <em>Tarenaya</em> (Tr-α). The Br-α and Tr-α events are of approximately the same age, allowing us to contrast independent ancient hexaploidy events from closely related lineages. The analysis of the retention of replicated genes (syntelogs) of the Type II MADS box genes provides a compelling example of what can be deduced by the comparison of these independent ancient triplications. We found that <em>Brassica</em> retains nearly twice as many Type-II MADS box genes as does <em>Tarenaya</em>. Genes retained after polyploidy often are dosage-sensitive gene complexes, whereby interacting partners must be maintained in the proper ratios (<a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Edger and Pires, 2009</a>). Considering the wealth of phenotypic diversity seen in the <em>Brassica</em> crops, we hypothesize that this great enrichment of morphological regulators in Brassica derived from Br-α may play a significant role. Also, the recently released genome of <em>L.</em> <em>alabamica</em> of the Brassicaceae also revealed an ancient hexaploidy (La-α) (<a id="xref-ref-34-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Haudry et al. 2013</a>). Future comparisons of the closely related La-α, Br-α, and Th-α events should be fruitful.</p><p id="p-49">Comparative analyses can also be used to identify important gene transposition and deletion events. Type-II MADS box genes are remarkably resistant to gene transpositions and thus their collinearity is highly conserved across all angiosperms (Type-I MADS box genes are highly prone to transposition, but their functions are less known). When comparing <em>Tarenaya</em> to Brassicaceae, we found almost all Type-II MADS box genes are collinear, except for the B-class homologs of <em>AP3</em> and <em>PI</em>. Specifically, we established that there was a Brassicaceae-specific transposition of <em>AP3</em> and a rare tandem duplication of a floral MADS box gene of <em>AP3</em> homologs in <em>Tarenaya</em>. The transposition of <em>AP3</em> also involved a neighboring Forkhead gene, which may be coregulated and important for <em>AP3</em> function. We further demonstrate that <em>Tarenaya</em> has two homologs of <em>PI</em>: one that is syntenic with other eudicots and for which the locus is lost from Brassicaceae and one that is syntenic with Brassicaceae <em>PI</em> homologs. Both <em>PI</em> and <em>AP3</em> in <em>Tarenaya</em> have diverged in expression levels. MADS box B-class gene homologs in the <em>AP3</em> lineage as well as <em>TCP</em> members have been implicated in the establishment of monosymmetric flowers in monocots, including Orchidaceae species (<a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">Tsai et al., 2004</a>, <a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Mondragón-Palomino and Theissen, 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Bartlett and Specht, 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Preston and Hileman, 2012</a>), <em>PI</em> genes have contributed to floral diversification in Asterids (<a id="xref-ref-98-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">Viaene et al., 2009</a>), and B-class genes have contributed to <em>Aquilegia</em> floral diversification (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Kramer et al., 2007</a>). Thus, it is possible that both B-class and TCP genes have an impact on floral monosymmetry in Cleomaceae. Furthermore, B-class gene diversification has also been implicated in regulating floral gender shifts (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Ackerman et al., 2008</a>) and could similarly have diversified in Cleomaceae.</p><p id="p-50">By comparing Brassicaceae genomes with the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome, we established that <em>SRK</em> in the S-locus occurred via a local rearrangement/duplication of an <em>ARK3</em>-like gene and subsequent expansion and diversification in Brassicaceae. In <em>Tarenaya</em>, we can clearly identify syntenic regions that contain homologous functional genes, including an <em>ARK3</em> and <em>CRK</em> homologs. The exact function of <em>ARK3</em> is not known, but based on gene expression analysis, this gene is thought to function during development of the sporophyte, perhaps in processes related to organ maturation, the establishment of growth pattern transitions (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Dwyer et al., 1994</a>), and/or involvement in pathogen responses (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Pastuglia et al., 2002</a>). However, it is not expressed in the stigma. Further research on Brassicaceae and Cleomaceae <em>ARK3</em> homologs is needed to establish the function of these genes. Interestingly, while Cleomaceae species do not have a <a id="xref-def-1-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> system, many species, including <em>T. hassleriana</em>, are polygamous (trimonoecious) and can have flowers on the same inflorescence with different genders: male sterile, female sterile, or complete (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">Stout, 1923</a>; <a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Cruden and Lloyd, 1995</a>; <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Machado et al., 2006</a>), providing an alternative mechanism to reduce inbreeding.</p><p id="p-51">Our results demonstrate the utility of the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome in complementing Brassicaceae genetic research in efforts to understand the function and evolution of genes and traits. The <em>Tarenaya</em> genome sequence will also pave the way for further studies of Cleomaceae traits not found in Brassicaceae, such as the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Brown et al., 2005</a>; <a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Marshall et al., 2007</a>).</p></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-11"><h2 class="">METHODS</h2><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Sample Preparation, Library Construction, Genome Sequencing, and Assembly</h3><p id="p-52">The purple-flowered <em>Tarenaya hassleriana</em> (Purple Queen) line selected for sequencing (ES1100) was first inbred by hand-pollination and floral bagging for four generations. Earlier generations of this line have previously been used for both BAC library construction and limited BAC sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-77-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Schranz and Mitchell-Olds, 2006</a>) and transcriptome sequencing and analysis (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Barker et al., 2009</a>); however, the material was referred to as being from <em>Cleome spinosa</em>. The two species are morphologically very similar, with only slight differences in stem-spine morphology, pubescence of sepals, ovary and capsules, and flower color; <em>C. spinosa</em> has only white flowers and the sepal and ovary are glandular-pubescent, whereas <em>T. hassleriana</em> can be white, pink, or purple and the sepals and ovary are glabrous (have no pubescence). Thus, many commercial seed providers erroneously label their <em>T. hassleriana</em> material as <em>C. spinosa</em>.</p><p id="p-53">We extracted DNA from leaves of <em>T. hassleriana</em> and constructed seven pair-end libraries with insert sizes of 350 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, and 20 kb. Illumina Hisequation 2000 was then applied to sequence those DNA libraries, and 70.22-Gb raw data were generated. Low-quality reads, reads with adaptor sequences, and duplicated reads were filtered, and the remaining high quality data were used in the assembly. SOAPdenovo2.21 was applied to assemble the genome in the procedure of contig construction, scaffold construction, and gap closure. After gap closure, the assembly was broken down into contigs again according to the position of Ns in the assembly.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3><a id="xref-def-2-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> and Physical Map Construction</h3><p id="p-54">The BAC library was prepared using leaf material of <em>T. hassleriana</em>. Two BAC libraries were subsequently generated: the first using <em>Hin</em>dIII (CLEH library) and the second using <em>Eco</em>RI (CLEE library). Average insert sizes were 145 kb for the CLEH library and 130 kb for the CLEE library. The vector used for library construction was pCC1BAC (Epicentre). For each library, 9600 clones were picked and arrayed into 384 well plates. Together, the two libraries equal ∼8.8 genome equivalents (4.6 GE CLEH library and 4.2 GE CLEE library) at an estimated haploid genome size of 300 Mb. <a id="xref-def-2-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a> was performed according to the methods detailed by <a id="xref-ref-96-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">van Oeveren et al. (2011)</a>. The resulting FPC map was used in further analysis.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>FPC Map Assembly and Integration with de Novo Assembled Scaffolds</h3><p id="p-55">Sequence-based physical BAC maps were assembled using an improved version of FPC software (Keygene), capable of processing sequence-based BAC fingerprint (<a id="xref-def-2-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">WGP</a>) data instead of fragment mobility information as used in the original FPC software (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">Soderlund et al., 1997</a>). The scaffolds from the SOAPdenovo assembly were then mapped to the physical contigs using nucleotide BLAST (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Altschul et al., 1997</a>). Hits were used only when they had a 100% identity match to the anchors. Subsequent filtering was performed to eliminate anchors with multiple hits and to establish superscaffold strand direction. The scaffolds were then ordered according to the mapped anchors and reassembled into superscaffolds.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>RNA-Seq</h3><p id="p-56">A mixed sample from five tissues (buds, leaves, petioles, stems, and flowers) from <em>Tarenaya</em> flowering plants was used to isolate RNA. Total RNA was extracted using Trizol (Invitrogen). The isolated RNA was then treated with RNase-Free DNase and then subsequently with an Illumina mRNA-Seq Prep Kit, following the manufacturer’s instructions. The insert size of the RNA libraries was ∼200 bp, and the sequencing was done using Illumina GA II. Raw reads were filtered if there were adaptor contaminations and low quality (&gt;10% bases with unknown quality). After filtering, all RNA reads were mapped back to the reference genome using Tophat Version 1.3.3 (<a id="xref-ref-90-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">Trapnell et al., 2009</a>), implemented with bowtie66 version 0.12.7 (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Langmead, 2010</a>), and the transcripts were assembled according to the genome using Cufflinks (version 1.1.0) (<a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Trapnell et al., 2012</a>). Single libraries from eight different tissues were isolated with the Qiagen RNeasy plant mini kit and treated with RNase-free DNase. Illumina TruSeq Libraries were constructed according to the manufacturer’s suggestions and sequenced. Raw reads were filtered to remove adaptors and low-quality bases and mapped to the predicted coding sequences using Cufflinks.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Plant Genome Sources</h3><p id="p-57">In all analyses where plant genomes are used, source database and version information can be found in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 18</a> online.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Annotation</h3><p id="p-58">Gene models were predicted following several steps: (1) De novo gene prediction. De novo predictions were performed on repeat masked genome assembly. AUGUSTUS (version 2.03) (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Stanke and Morgenstern, 2005</a>), GlimmerHMM (version 3.02) (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Majoros et al., 2004</a>), and SNAP (version 2.0) were used to perform the de novo annotation. (2) Homology gene prediction. The protein sequences from <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, <em>Brassica rapa</em>, <em>Carica papaya</em>, <em>Glycine max</em>, Theobroma cacao, and <em>Vitis vinifera</em> were mapped to the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome using tBLASTn, by an E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>, and then Genewise (version 2.2.0) (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Birney et al., 2004</a>) was used for gene annotation. (3) RNA aided annotation. All the RNA reads were mapped back to the reference genome by Tophat (version 1.0.14) (<a id="xref-ref-90-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">Trapnell et al., 2009</a>), implemented with bowtie version 0.12.5, and the transcripts were assembled according to the genome using Cufflinks (version 0.8.2) (<a id="xref-ref-91-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">Trapnell et al., 2012</a>). All the predictions were combined using GLEAN to produce the consensus gene sets.</p><p id="p-59">The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Lowe and Eddy, 1997</a>). For rRNA identification, the <em>Arabidopsis</em> rRNA sequences were first downloaded from the National Center for Biotechnology Information (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore</a>). Then, rRNAs in the database were aligned against the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome using BLASTn to identify possible rRNAs. Other noncoding RNAs, including microRNA and small nuclear RNA, were identified using INFERNAL (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Nawrocki et al., 2009</a>) by searching against the Rfam database.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Family Clustering</h3><p id="p-60">OrthoMCL (version 1.4) (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Li et al., 2003</a>) was used with default parameters followed by an all-versus-all BLASTP (E-value ≤ 1e-5) process using the protein sequence data sets from six plant species. Splice variants were removed from the data set (the longest protein sequence prediction was usually maintained), and the internal stop codons and incompatible reading frames were filtered. All gene families were classified according to the presence or absence of genes for specific species, and it was determined which gene families were species specific or genus specific.</p><p id="p-61">A total of 184204 sequences from <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, <em>C. papaya</em>, <em>Vitis vinifera</em>, and <em>T. hassleriana</em> were clustered into 24,591 gene families. Of these, 9395 contained sequences from all six genomes, 2492 from Brassicaceae (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>B. rapa</em>), 1176 from plants as outgroups only bearing the At-γ event (<em>C. papaya</em> and <em>V. vinifera</em>), and 748 clusters were specific to <em>Tarenaya</em>. Of the 28,917 protein-coding genes predicted for <em>Tarenaya</em>, 22,482 were clustered in a total of 14,505 groups. The 748 <em>Tarenaya</em>-specific clusters contained 1556 genes, of which 529 have at least one INTERPRO domain. Singletons make up a total of 6435 genes, of which 2926 have at least one INTERPRO domain. Interestingly, many gene families that have decreased in number of genes in <em>Tarenaya</em> show bigger genes than others, containing more exons and more <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">TE</a> insertions. However, more Gene Ontology annotations are enriched for these contraction gene families. These results indicated that these contraction gene families may be functionally constrained.</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Repeat Annotation</h3><p id="p-62">Repeats of the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome were identified by a combination of homology-based and de novo approaches. In the homology-based method, databases of known repetitive sequences were used to search against the genome assembly. In this way, RepeatMasker-3.2.9 and RepeatProteinMask software (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">Chen, 2004</a>) were used to build the homology database and search the repeat sequence.</p><p id="p-63">Furthermore, in the de novo approach, three de novo software packages (Piler-DF-1.0 [<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Edgar and Myers, 2005</a>], RepeatScout-1.0.5, and LTR-FINDER-1.0.5 [<a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">Xu and Wang, 2007</a>]) were used to build a de novo repeat database of the <em>Tarenaya</em> genome. RepeatMasker was then used to identify repeats using both the repeat database that we built in-house and Repbase. Finally, the de novo prediction and the homolog prediction of <a id="xref-def-3-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">TEs</a> according to the position in the genome were combined.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Analysis and Species Divergence Time Estimation</h3><p id="p-64">The maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree of <em>T. hassleriana</em> and other plant genomes was constructed using whole-genome fourfold degenerate sites among species. <em>Oryza sativa</em> and <em>Sorghum bicolor</em> were taken as the monocot outgroups. The following steps were taken: First, all of the single-copy gene families were extracted from the OrthoMCL clustering results. Second, multiple sequence alignments were run for each single copy gene family using the protein-coding sequences. Third, for each aligned gene family, the CDS back-translation of the protein multiple alignments was performed from the original DNA sequences using in-house Perl scripts, and then the fourfold degenerate sites of orthologous genes in all single-copy gene families (concatenated into one supergene for each species) were extracted. The branch length represents the neutral divergence rate. The substitution model (GTR+gamma+I) and Mrbayes (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Ronquist et al., 2012</a>) were used to reconstruct the phylogenetic tree.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Synteny and Collinearity Analysis</h3><p id="p-65">First, a homology search within and between species was performed using BLASTP (E-value threshold 1e-7, top 20 hits). Tandem gene families and weak matches were removed using in-house Perl scripts for further analysis. Tandem gene families were defined as clusters of genes within 10 intervening genes from one another, and the longest model was maintained to represent each family. For the weak matches, only the top BLAST hits were retained by applying a C-score threshold of 0.8 [C-score(A, B) = score(A, B)/max(best score of A, best score of B)] (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Putnam et al., 2007</a>).</p><p id="p-66">Then, based on these filtered BLAST results, the whole genome-wide sequence alignments within and between genomes using genes as anchors, which was to search syntenic blocks, were conducted by an in-house pipeline implementing Dynamic Programming (parameters: score_of_match, 50; penalty of mismatch, −5; penalty of indel, −5; penalty_of_extension_indel, −2; block_size, ≥5 gene pairs; gap_between_neighbor_blocks, 30 genes). The running time for each whole-genome sequence alignment using genes as anchors by Dynamic programming was ∼5 to ∼6 h. At the same time, i-Adhore 3.0 (<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Proost et al., 2012</a>) (<a href="http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/software">http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/software</a>) was used to identify syntenic and collinearity blocks (gap_size = 30, cluster_gap = 35, q_value = 0.75, prob_cutoff = 0.01, anchor_points = 5, alignment_method = gg4, level_2_only = false, table_type = family) within and between genomes, and all the syntenic blocks identified by i-adhore were contained in our results identified by the Dynamic programming method; however, the latter method is more sensitive and accurate. All the dot plot figures were plotted using a Support Vector Graphics package implemented perl scripts (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 1, 2, 6 to 9, and 11 to 14</a> online).</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>Ancestral Genome Reconstruction</h3><p id="p-67">Based on the paralogous duplicates within each genome, four minimized genomes were independently created for <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, and <em>T. hassleriana</em>, by condensing local duplications to one gene, removing transposons, and including only genes within blocks defined by retained pairs. Each of the minimized genomes represents the ancestral state and predate the recent polyploidy event. At the same time, these four ancestral state genomes were compared with the Ken Wolfe’s 45 ancestral blocks of <em>A. thaliana</em> (see <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 11 to 14</a> online).</p></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h3>Partition of the <em>T. hassleriana</em> Genome into Three Subgenomes Following the Recent Polyploidy Event</h3><p id="p-68">To avoid the potentially confounding results with the independent ancient polyploidy events, the ancestor genome of <em>A. lyrata</em> (A ancestor) was used as the reference, and collinear blocks were identified using i-adhore 3.0 by aligning the four proteomes (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, and <em>T. hassleriana</em>) against the A ancestor genome. From the last common ancestor (the A ancestor represents this genome state) of these four species, both <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> experienced a whole-genome duplication event (At-α), <em>B. rapa</em> experienced one whole-genome duplication event (At-α) and an additional whole-genome triplication event (Br-α), and <em>T. hassleriana</em> experienced an independently whole-genome triplication event (Th-α). Therefore, quote ratios of 2:1, 2:1, 3/4/5/6:1, and 3:1 were observed for <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, and <em>T. hassleriana</em> genomes. For <em>T. hassleriana</em>, the triplicated blocks identified were chained into three subgenomes compared with the A ancestor using dynamic programming. The main criteria are that the chained triplicated blocks are (1) nonoverlapping in the <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome; (2) have no more than 10% overlap between their orthologous A ancestor regions (results were similar using 0% overlap in A ancestor); and (3) maximize coverage of the <em>T. hassleriana</em> genome (annotated gene space). A similar strategy was taken for other genomes based on their polyploidy level from the last recent common ancestor, as shown in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Figures 16 to 19</a> online. For <em>T. hassleriana</em>, a total of 16,770 (63.2%) <em>Tarenaya</em> genes are in the triplicated blocks compared with the A ancestor, 688 (2.6%) genes are in the duplicated blocks, which indicate that another copy was possibly lost after the triplication event, and only 135 (0.5%) have one syntenic ortholog, which means that a very small subset of triplicated genes lost two copies. However, 8913 (33.6%) <em>Tarenaya</em> genes are not in any replicated blocks, which indicates species-specific deletion in <em>A. lyrata</em> or species-specific gains in <em>Tarenaya</em> after their divergence along evolutionary time.</p></div><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h3>Interproscan and Gene Functional Annotation</h3><p id="p-69">Interproscan version 4.5 was used to scan protein sequences against the protein signatures from InterPro (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Hunter et al., 2009</a>) (version 22.0) to infer functions for the protein-coding genes. This was done for the entire target proteomes involved in our main text analysis, including <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>T. hassleriana</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, <em>Solanum lycopersicum</em>, <em>V. vinifera</em>, <em>Prunus persica</em>, <em>Populus trichocarpa</em>, and <em>C. papaya</em>. InterPro integrates protein families, domains, and functional sites from different databases: Pfam, PROSITE, PRINTS, ProDom, SMART, TIGRFAMs, PIRSF, SUPERFAMILY, Gene3D, and PANTHER. Interproscan integrates the searching algorithms of all these databases. In total, Interproscan identified 93,038 protein domains of 4733 distinct domain types. Seventy-five percent of the genes (21,829 out of 28,917 genes in total) have been assigned with at least one domain.</p></div><div id="sec-25" class="subsection other1"><h3>Genomic Analysis for Reproductive Traits</h3><p id="p-70">For the syntenic and protein domain analysis of <a id="xref-def-1-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SI</a> genes, the genes annotated in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>A. lyrata</em> were used as published in an extensive study into S-locus variation in <em>Arabidopsis</em> species (<a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Guo et al., 2011</a>). Homologs were then sought using top BLAST hits of these genes against <em>T. hassleriana</em> and <em>B. rapa</em>. All homology candidates were confirmed by manual inspection of the alignment in dot plots generated in MAFFT (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Katoh et al., 2002</a>) to confirm synteny of candidate regions. After compiling a definitive list of syntenic regions, the genes from these regions were analyzed through the PFAM online protein domain analysis program (<a id="xref-ref-69-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Punta et al., 2012</a>). <a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Figure 6</a> was manually compiled from the results of this program.</p></div><div id="sec-26" class="subsection"><h3><a id="xref-def-4-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">qRT-PCR</a></h3><p id="p-71">For the qRT-PCR, total RNA was isolated from roots, leaves, three bud stages (1 to 5 mm, 5 to 10 mm, and 10 to 25 mm length), sepals, petals, stamens, carpels at anthesis, and three stages of siliques (10 mm, 10 to 30 mm, and 30 to 50 mm length) with the GeneJET plant RNA purification mini kit (Fermentas). First-strand cDNA was synthesized using 500 ng total RNA with the RevertAid H Minus First Strand cDNA synthesis kit (Fermentas) using random hexamer primers.</p><p id="p-72">The <a id="xref-def-4-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">qRT-PCR</a> experiments were performed according to the MIQE guidelines (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Bustin et al., 2009</a>). Exon spanning primers were generated using PerlPrimer 1.1.21. (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Marshall, 2004</a>). A primer efficiency test was performed and the primers were tested with genomic DNA to ensure cDNA specificity. Standard dose response curves were constructed for all genes using serial dilutions (1:50 to 1:50,000) of 10- to 25-mm-long bud cDNA template to calculate amplification efficiency. The <a id="xref-def-4-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">qRT-PCR</a> assay was performed with the LightCycler 480 II (Roche) and the data analyzed with the LCS480 1.5.0.39 software. Each reaction was composed of 10 μL of 2× DyNAmo Flash SYBR Green Mastermix (Biozym Scientific), 2 μL each of 10 μM forward and reverse primers, 1 μL water, and 5 μL of 1:100 diluted template cDNA. Each reaction was performed in biological duplicate and technical triplicate along with water and RNA controls for each primer pair. The <em>GLYCERALDEHYDE-3-PHOSPHATE DEHYDROGENASE C SUBUNIT</em> and <em>ELONGATION FACTOR 1-ALPHA</em> genes served as internal controls. The following PCR program was used: 7 min at 95°C; 45 cycles of 10 s at 95°C, 15 s at 60°C, and 15 s at 72°C, followed by a melting curve of 5 s at 95°C, 1 min at 65°C, and 30 s at 97°C. The quantification cycles (C<sub>q</sub>) were calculated according to the second derivative maximum algorithm. The raw C<sub>q</sub> data were analyzed according to the Comparitive C<sub>q</sub> method (ΔΔC<sub>q</sub>) (<a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">Schmittgen and Livak, 2008</a>). Gene expression was first normalized relative to the expression of the two reference genes in the respective tissues. The expression was further normalized with the expression of the reference genes in 10- to 25-mm long buds, which acted as an interassay calibrator. The relative expression was then calculated with reference to the expression of <em>T. hassleriana CAL</em> in stage 3 buds.</p></div><div id="sec-27" class="subsection"><h3>Accession Numbers</h3><p id="p-73">Sequence data from this article can be found in the Arabidopsis Genome Initiative or GenBank/EMBL databases under the following accession numbers: Th<em>-CAL/AP1-1</em>, Th01189; Th<em>-CAL/AP1-2</em>, Th13754; Th-<em>PI-1</em>, Th05675; Th-<em>PI-2</em>, Th17298; Th-<em>AP3-1</em>, Th02920; Th-<em>AP3-2</em>, Th02921; <em>EMB196</em>, AT3G54350; and <em>TCP1</em>, AT1G67260. The genomic reads of <em>T. hassleriana</em>, as well as RNA sequencing data, have been deposited into the <a id="xref-def-5-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">NCBI</a> Short Read Archive under accession numbers SRA058749 and GSM1008474. The information for the raw reads data can be found in <a href="http://www.plantcell.org/cgi/content/full/tpc.113.113480/DC1">Supplemental Table 1</a> online. The genome sequence and annotation data set have been deposited into <a id="xref-def-5-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">NCBI</a> (project ID PRJNA175230 [superscaffolds]; the accession number is AOUI00000000).</p></div><div id="sec-28" class="subsection"><h3>Supplemental Data</h3><p id="p-74">The following materials are available in the online version of this article.</p><ul G.Z., M.E.S., P.Z. and S.C. led the sequencing and analysis. C.S., Z.Z., W.L., M.L., Y.T., J.W., X.X, H.Z. and Z.Q. assisted with sequencing and analysis. J.C. and G.F. did the SOAPdenovo genome assembly. J.C. and X.Zhong. did the annotation. P.Z., S.C., E.v.d.B., M.E.S., and C.B. did the evolutionary analysis. S.C., J.X., E.v.d.B., and J.H. constructed the physical map. M.E.S., S.C., and E.v.d.B. did the reproductive trait evolution analysis. K.K. and S.d.B. did the phylogenetic analysis of MADS box genes. C.K., A.Bräutigam., and A.P.M.W. conducted tissue-specific transcriptomic analyses of <em>T. hassleriana</em>. A.Bräutigam. and A.Becker. did the <a id="xref-def-4-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">qRT-PCR</a> analysis of MADS box genes. J.C.H. did analysis of the <em>TCP</em> gene family. M.E.S., E.v.d.B., and S.C. wrote the article.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul><li class="fn" id="fn-2"><p id="p-2">The author responsible for distribution of materials integral to the findings presented in this article in accordance with the policy described in the Instructions for Authors (<a href="http://www.plantcell.org">www.plantcell.org</a>) is: M.
